---
title: In-context Learning and Induction Heads
href: https://transformer-circuits.pub/2022/in-context-learning-and-induction-heads/index.html
---

> [I]nduction heads might constitute the mechanism for the actual majority of all in-context learning in large transformer models.


**Thesis**: there are "fuzzy" induction heads that perform `[A*][B*] ... [A] -> [B]` where `A* ≈ A` and `B* ≈ B`.

**Evidence**: 
1. **Macroscopic co-occurrence**: A phase change occurs (visible as a bump in training loss) during which the model coincidentally acquires in-context learning ability & induction heads.
2. **Macroscopic co-perturbation**: Enabling one-layer models to learn induction heads substantially improves their performance. It also makes the transition earlier for models of all sizes. 
3. **Direct ablation**: Knocking out induction heads at test-time in small models greatly decreases in-context learning.
4. **Specific examples of induction head generality**
5. **Mechanistic plausibility of induction head generality**
6. **Continuity from small to large models**

**Glossary**
- **Induction heads**: `[A][B] ... [A] -> [B]`
	- **Properties**:
		- **Prefix matching**: head attends to previous tokens that were followed by the current token (/local context)
		- **Copying**: head increases the logit for the attended-to token
	- **Requirements**
		- **>1 layer**: to perform each of these properties.
	- This is an algorithm, not fixed statistics. It generalizes well out-of-distribution.
- **In-context learning**: As the context (prompt) gets larger, it gets easier to predict subsequent tokens.
	- ==How to measure?==
		- **Few-shot learning** ([[@brown2020]])
		- **Decreasing loss at increasing token indices** ([[@kaplan2020]]): specifically loss at token 500 minus loss at token 50.
		- > One of the stranger observations in this paper is that [in-context learning score](https://transformer-circuits.pub/2022/in-context-learning-and-induction-heads/index.html#in-context-learning-key-concept) (as we've defined it: the loss of the 500th token in the context minus the loss of the 50th token in the context) is more or less the same for all models after the phase change.... The only thing that matters, seemingly, is whether the model has gone through the phase change at all. ^f5c335
		- > What's going on is that the large models gain all their advantage over small models very early in the context. In fact, the majority of the difference forms in the first ten > The relationship between scaling laws and mechanistic interpretability might be seen as analogous to the relationship between thermodynamics and the physics of individual particles. For both thermodynamics and scaling laws, even though the underlying system is very complicated, we're able to find simple relationships between the variables – for thermodynamics: entropy, temperature, volume and pressure; for neural networks: loss, compute, parameters, and data. In contrast, mechanistic interpretability studies the individual circuits underlying our models, vaguely analogous to how one might carefully study individual particles in physics. In physics, these two layers of abstraction were bridged by statistical physics.
